<meta name="DC.Title" content="The resurrection genome of Boea hygrometrica: A blueprint for survival of dehydration" />
<meta name="DC.Identifier" content="10.1073/pnas.1505811112" />

<meta name="DC.Description" content="The genome analysis presented here represents a major step forward in the field of desiccation tolerance and a much-anticipated resource that will have a far-reaching effect in many areas of plant biology and agriculture. We present the ∼1.69-Gb draft genome of Boea hygrometrica , an important plant model for understanding responses to dehydration. To our knowledge, this is the first genome sequence of a desiccation-tolerant extremophile, offering insight into the evolution of this important trait and a first look, to our knowledge, into the genome organization of desiccation tolerance. The underpinning genome architecture and response in relation to the hydration state of the plant and its role in the preservation of cellular integrity has important implications for developing drought tolerance improvement strategies for our crops." />

<meta name="citation_title" content="The resurrection genome of Boea hygrometrica: A blueprint for survival of dehydration" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;“Drying without dying” is an essential trait in land plant evolution. Unraveling how a unique group of angiosperms, the Resurrection Plants, survive desiccation of their leaves and roots has been hampered by the lack of a foundational genome perspective. Here we report the ∼1,691-Mb sequenced genome of &lt;i&gt;Boea hygrometrica&lt;/i&gt;, an important resurrection plant model. The sequence revealed evidence for two historical genome-wide duplication events, a compliment of 49,374 protein-coding genes, 29.15% of which are unique (orphan) to &lt;i&gt;Boea&lt;/i&gt; and 20% of which (9,888) significantly respond to desiccation at the transcript level. Expansion of early light-inducible protein (ELIP) and 5S &lt;i&gt;rRNA&lt;/i&gt; genes highlights the importance of the protection of the photosynthetic apparatus during drying and the rapid resumption of protein synthesis in the resurrection capability of &lt;i&gt;Boea&lt;/i&gt;. Transcriptome analysis reveals extensive alternative splicing of transcripts and a focus on cellular protection strategies. The lack of desiccation tolerance-specific genome organizational features suggests the resurrection phenotype evolved mainly by an alteration in the control of dehydration response genes.&lt;/p&gt;" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" scheme="executive-summary" content="&lt;h3&gt;Significance&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The genome analysis presented here represents a major step forward in the field of desiccation tolerance and a much-anticipated resource that will have a far-reaching effect in many areas of plant biology and agriculture. We present the ∼1.69-Gb draft genome of &lt;i&gt;Boea hygrometrica&lt;/i&gt;, an important plant model for understanding responses to dehydration. To our knowledge, this is the first genome sequence of a desiccation-tolerant extremophile, offering insight into the evolution of this important trait and a first look, to our knowledge, into the genome organization of desiccation tolerance. The underpinning genome architecture and response in relation to the hydration state of the plant and its role in the preservation of cellular integrity has important implications for developing drought tolerance improvement strategies for our crops.&lt;/p&gt;" />

   </h1>

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_pnas_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_pnas_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="executive-summary"><h2 class="">Significance</h2><p id="p-4">The genome analysis presented here represents a major step forward in the field of desiccation tolerance and a much-anticipated resource that will have a far-reaching effect in many areas of plant biology and agriculture. We present the ∼1.69-Gb draft genome of <em>Boea hygrometrica</em>, an important plant model for understanding responses to dehydration. To our knowledge, this is the first genome sequence of a desiccation-tolerant extremophile, offering insight into the evolution of this important trait and a first look, to our knowledge, into the genome organization of desiccation tolerance. The underpinning genome architecture and response in relation to the hydration state of the plant and its role in the preservation of cellular integrity has important implications for developing drought tolerance improvement strategies for our crops.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-5">“Drying without dying” is an essential trait in land plant evolution. Unraveling how a unique group of angiosperms, the Resurrection Plants, survive desiccation of their leaves and roots has been hampered by the lack of a foundational genome perspective. Here we report the ∼1,691-Mb sequenced genome of <em>Boea hygrometrica</em>, an important resurrection plant model. The sequence revealed evidence for two historical genome-wide duplication events, a compliment of 49,374 protein-coding genes, 29.15% of which are unique (orphan) to <em>Boea</em> and 20% of which (9,888) significantly respond to desiccation at the transcript level. Expansion of early light-inducible protein (ELIP) and 5S <em>rRNA</em> genes highlights the importance of the protection of the photosynthetic apparatus during drying and the rapid resumption of protein synthesis in the resurrection capability of <em>Boea</em>. Transcriptome analysis reveals extensive alternative splicing of transcripts and a focus on cellular protection strategies. The lack of desiccation tolerance-specific genome organizational features suggests the resurrection phenotype evolved mainly by an alteration in the control of dehydration response genes.</p></div><ul class="kwd-group"><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/vegetative-desiccation-tolerance" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-vegetative-desiccation-tolerance" rel="nofollow">vegetative desiccation tolerance</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/resurrection-plant" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-resurrection-plant" rel="nofollow">resurrection plant</a></li><li class="kwd"><a xmlns:default="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" href="/keyword/boea-hygrometrica" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-boea-hygrometrica" rel="nofollow"><em xmlns:default="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml">Boea hygrometrica</em></a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/drought-tolerance-enhancement" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-drought-tolerance-enhancement" rel="nofollow">drought tolerance enhancement</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/genome" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-genome" rel="nofollow">genome</a></li></ul><p id="p-6" class="flushleft">Resurrection plants constitute a unique cadre within the angiosperms: they alone have the remarkable capability to survive the complete dehydration of their leaves and roots. How the dry and visually “dead” plants come alive when water becomes available has long fascinated plant biologists and the lay public alike. The majority of plants, including all our crops, can rarely survive tissue water potentials of less than −4 Mpa. Resurrection plants can, in contrast, survive tissue water potentials of −100 MPa (equilibration to air of 50% relative humidity) and below. The ability to desiccate and resurrect vegetative tissues is considered a primal strategy for surviving extensive periods of drought (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>). Desiccation tolerance (DT) has played a major role in plant evolution (<a id="xref-ref-1-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>): Postulated as critical for the colonization of terrestrial habitats. DT, as it relates to seed survival and storage, is also arguably the primary plant trait that governs global agriculture and food security. Vegetative DT was lost early in the evolution of tracheophytes (<a id="xref-ref-1-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>) and is rare in the angiosperms, but has since reappeared within several lineages, at least 13 of which belong to the angiosperms (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>).</p><p id="p-7">Vegetative DT is a complex multigenic and multifactorial phenotype (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a><a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-4"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>), but understanding how DT plants respond to and survive dehydration has great significance for plant biology and, more directly, for agriculture. Resurrection plants offer a potential source of genes for improvement of crop drought tolerance (<a id="xref-ref-5-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>, <a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>) as the demand for fresh water grows (<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>).</p><p id="p-8">In recent decades, efforts have been focused on exploring the structural, physiologic, and molecular aspects of DT in a number of plant species (<a id="xref-ref-4-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>). Although a functional genomic approach has been fruitful in revealing the intricacies of DT in resurrection plants (<a id="xref-ref-5-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>, <a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>), and a system approach is contemplated (<a id="xref-ref-4-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>), efforts are hampered by the lack of a sequenced genome for any of the resurrection plants. To fill this critical gap, we sequenced the genome of one of the important DT models (<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>), <em>Boea hygrometrica.</em></p><p id="p-9"><em>B. hygrometrica</em> is a homiochlorophyllous dicot in Gesneriaceae that grows in rocky areas throughout most of China (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>). Not only is the whole plant DT (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>A</em></a>), but a detached leaf or leaf segment retains the DT phenotype and can regenerate a new “seedling” even after several dehydration and rehydration cycles (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>B</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S1 <em>A</em> and <em>B</em></a>) (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>). Drying leaf tissues exhibit classical dehydration-associated structural changes (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>), including a folded cell wall and condensed cytoplasm (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S1 <em>C</em>–<em>E</em></a>).</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-featured  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/18/5833/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Phenotypes during the dry&#x2013;rehydration cycle and life cycle of B. hygrometrica. (A) Vegetative phenotypes of hydrated (HD), dry (2 weeks withholding water), and rehydrated for 48 h (RH) B. hygrometrica. (B) Life cycle of B. hygrometrica from seed germination or leaf regeneration to mature plant. (Scale bar for seed morphology, 1 mm; scale bar for plants, 1 cm.)" class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1612957475" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Phenotypes during the dry&#x2013;rehydration cycle and life cycle of &lt;em&gt;B. hygrometrica&lt;/em&gt;. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) Vegetative phenotypes of hydrated (HD), dry (2 weeks withholding water), and rehydrated for 48 h (RH) &lt;em&gt;B. hygrometrica&lt;/em&gt;. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Life cycle of &lt;em&gt;B. hygrometrica&lt;/em&gt; from seed germination or leaf regeneration to mature plant. (Scale bar for seed morphology, 1 mm; scale bar for plants, 1 cm.)&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 1." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/18/5833/F1.medium.gif" width="438" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 1." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/18/5833/F1.medium.gif" width="438" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/18/5833/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 1." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/18/5833/F1.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/487366" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 1.</span> <p id="p-10" class="first-child">Phenotypes during the dry–rehydration cycle and life cycle of <em>B. hygrometrica</em>. (<em>A</em>) Vegetative phenotypes of hydrated (HD), dry (2 weeks withholding water), and rehydrated for 48 h (RH) <em>B. hygrometrica</em>. (<em>B</em>) Life cycle of <em>B. hygrometrica</em> from seed germination or leaf regeneration to mature plant. (Scale bar for seed morphology, 1 mm; scale bar for plants, 1 cm.)</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-11">Here we present a high-quality draft genome of <em>B. hygrometrica</em>, along with a full assessment of the changes in the leaf transcriptomes that occur during desiccation and that relate to the resurrection phenotype.</p><div class="section results" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">Results</h2><div id="sec-2" class="subsection"><h3>Whole-Genome Features.</h3><p id="p-12">The whole-genome shotgun sequenced draft genome of <em>B. hygrometrica</em> delivers a ∼1,548-Mb assembly, generated from 4.74 × 10<sup>11</sup> high-quality reads (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S1</a>), and represents 91.52% of the ∼1,691-Mb estimated genome size (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S2</a>) predicted from 17-nucleotide depth distribution (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S2 and Table S3</a>). The assembly was generated by an iterative hybrid approach (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S3</a>). Approximately 85.86% of the assembly is nongapped sequence. The quality of the assembly was assessed by alignment to Sanger-derived fosmid sequences, allowing only a limited potential for misassemblies (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S4</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd01.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S1</a>). The extent of sequence coverage was confirmed by the mapping of 2,360 sequenced expressed sequence tags (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S5</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd02.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>).</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/487011/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/487011/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 1.</span> <p id="p-13" class="first-child">Overview of assembly and annotation for the <em>B. hygrometrica</em> draft genomes</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-14">The fourfold degenerate synonymous site of the third codon position (4DTv) values for coding regions for each of the duplicate gene pairs in the pairwise orthologous segments within <em>B. hygrometrica</em> genome revealed two whole-genome duplication events (4DTv ∼0.5 and ∼1.0; <a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>A</em></a>). The species divergence event between <em>B. hygrometrica</em> and <em>Solanum tuberosum</em> or <em>Solanum lycopersicum</em> (4DTv ∼0.54 or 0.49) that occurred around the most recent duplication event in the <em>B. hygrometrica</em> genome (4DTv ∼0.5) likely reflects the divergence of the Lamiales from the Solanales (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>A</em></a>). The ancient duplications, composed of several intermittent small duplication events (4DTv ∼0.9 to ∼1.3), may explain the large genome size, high level of repetitive sequences, and multicopy genes in the <em>B. hygrometrica</em> genome. The <em>B. hygrometrica</em> genome possessed a higher guanine-cytosine (GC) content (42.30%) than <em>S. tuberosum</em>, <em>S. lycopersicum</em>, or <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-2" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S4</a>), which is close to the upper limit for dicots (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>). More than three fourths of the genome is composed of repeat sequences (75.75% of the assembled genome; <a id="xref-table-wrap-1-3" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S5 and Table S6</a>), which is similar to other dicots (<a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">14</a>) but somewhat higher than <em>S. tuberosum</em> (62.2%) (<a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>). Much of the unassembled genome is also composed of repetitive sequences, and the majority of the repetitive sequences could not be associated with known transposable element families. Plant transposable elements (TEs) are a significant source of small RNAs that function to epigenetically regulate TE and gene activity and are known to regulate DT in dicots (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>). A recently discovered retroelement expressed in <em>B. hygrometrica</em>, osmotic and alkaline resistance 1, strengthens the possible role for LTRs in stress tolerance, and perhaps DT (<a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>).</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/18/5833/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="B. hygrometrica genome features. (A) Genome duplication in genomes of B. hygrometrica, S. tuberosum, and S. lycopersicum, as revealed through 4DTv analyses. (B) A Venn diagram illustrating shared and specific gene families and genes (within brackets) in B. hygrometrica, S. tuberosum, and S. lycopersicum. The gene family and its related number of genes are listed in each of the components. (C) A Venn diagram of gene set. (D) Profiles integrating genome structures with DEGs of the longest 10 scaffolds. (a&#x2013;d) Scaffolds indicating the distribution of ORFs (a, in blue), repetitive sequences with DNA II and RNA transposon (b, in yellow and orange), and DEG distribution on scaffolds in HD vs. 70% RWC and HD vs. 10% RWC (c, pink, accumulating DEGs; d, green, declining DEGs)." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1612957475" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;B. hygrometrica&lt;/em&gt; genome features. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) Genome duplication in genomes of &lt;em&gt;B. hygrometrica&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;S. tuberosum&lt;/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;S. lycopersicum&lt;/em&gt;, as revealed through 4DTv analyses. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) A Venn diagram illustrating shared and specific gene families and genes (within brackets) in &lt;em&gt;B. hygrometrica&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;S. tuberosum&lt;/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;S. lycopersicum&lt;/em&gt;. The gene family and its related number of genes are listed in each of the components. (&lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;) A Venn diagram of gene set. (&lt;em&gt;D&lt;/em&gt;) Profiles integrating genome structures with DEGs of the longest 10 scaffolds. (&lt;em&gt;a&#x2013;d&lt;/em&gt;) Scaffolds indicating the distribution of ORFs (&lt;em&gt;a&lt;/em&gt;, in blue), repetitive sequences with DNA II and RNA transposon (&lt;em&gt;b&lt;/em&gt;, in yellow and orange), and DEG distribution on scaffolds in HD vs. 70% RWC and HD vs. 10% RWC (&lt;em&gt;c&lt;/em&gt;, pink, accumulating DEGs; &lt;em&gt;d&lt;/em&gt;, green, declining DEGs).&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 2." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/18/5833/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="264"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 2." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/18/5833/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="264"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/18/5833/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 2." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/18/5833/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/487088" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 2.</span> <p id="p-15" class="first-child"><em>B. hygrometrica</em> genome features. (<em>A</em>) Genome duplication in genomes of <em>B. hygrometrica</em>, <em>S. tuberosum</em>, and <em>S. lycopersicum</em>, as revealed through 4DTv analyses. (<em>B</em>) A Venn diagram illustrating shared and specific gene families and genes (within brackets) in <em>B. hygrometrica</em>, <em>S. tuberosum</em>, and <em>S. lycopersicum</em>. The gene family and its related number of genes are listed in each of the components. (<em>C</em>) A Venn diagram of gene set. (<em>D</em>) Profiles integrating genome structures with DEGs of the longest 10 scaffolds. (<em>a–d</em>) Scaffolds indicating the distribution of ORFs (<em>a</em>, in blue), repetitive sequences with DNA II and RNA transposon (<em>b</em>, in yellow and orange), and DEG distribution on scaffolds in HD vs. 70% RWC and HD vs. 10% RWC (<em>c</em>, pink, accumulating DEGs; <em>d</em>, green, declining DEGs).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-16">The draft genome also encodes 196 microRNA (miRNA), 538 tRNA, 1,512 <em>rRNA</em>, and 151 snRNA genes (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S7</a>). In comparison with other dicot genomes (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a>), the <em>B. hygrometrica</em> genome encodes a large number of <em>rRNA</em> genes, especially 5S <em>rRNA</em> genes. Apart from their obvious structural role in ribosomes, large numbers of <em>rRNA</em> repeats (rDNA) have been linked with DNA stability, at least in yeast (<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>): a function that would be advantageous for surviving desiccation. There are 1,119 5S <em>rRNA</em> genes interspersed throughout the genome. This is 25–50 times the number contained in the only two other Asterid genomes that have been sequenced: <em>S. lycopersicum</em> (47 5S <em>rRNA</em> genes) and <em>S. tuberosum</em> (23 5S <em>rRNA</em> genes). The majority of the 5S <em>rRNA</em> genes are interspersed throughout the genome (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd03.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S3</a>); only 34 were clustered in four scaffolds (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S6</a>).</p><p id="p-17">Gene prediction protocols revealed 49,374 protein-coding genes, 40.68% of which are supported by RNA-Seq data and 23,250 (47.09%) of which had sufficient similarity to database entries to tentatively assign gene function (see <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S8</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Results</em></a> for details). The structural features of the protein-coding gene complements for <em>B. hygrometrica</em> were closely comparable to those reported for <em>S. tuberosum</em> and <em>S. lycopersicum</em> but differed substantially from those reported for <em>Arabidopsis</em> (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S7 and Table S8</a>). Of the predicted 12,269 potential gene families, 9,638 (∼78.56%), involving 14,218 genes, are shared with <em>S. tuberosum</em> and <em>S. lycopersicum</em> genomes, reflecting the common origin between Lamiales and Solanales in asterids (<a id="xref-fig-2-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>B</em></a>).</p><p id="p-18">Predicted genes were functionally annotated by a consensus approach, using InterPro (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>), Gene Ontology (GO), Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) (<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a>), Swissprot, and Translated EMBL Nucleotide Sequence Data Library (TrEMBL) (<a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>). The largest number of genes exhibited homology with proteins in the TrEMBL (46.12%) and InterPro (37.71%) databases (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S9</a>). In total, 23,250 genes (47.09%) had sufficient similarity to database entries to tentatively assign gene function. Of the annotated protein-coding genes, multicopy genes outnumber single-copy genes by a factor of two (<a id="xref-fig-2-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>C</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd04.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S4</a>). Both categories contain an almost equal number of genes contained in TEs and genes classified as orphans [genes that are not a member of a gene family and have no significant sequence similarity to any entry in protein databases outside the taxon of interest (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>)]. Up to 97% of the orphan genes originated from duplication events (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S10</a>).</p><p id="p-19">Of the genes that are historically associated with DT, in the <em>Boea</em> genome, only the early light-inducible protein (ELIP) gene family exhibits evidence of expansion. <em>B. hygrometrica</em> has seventeen ELIP genes (15 <em>ELIP1</em> and two <em>ELIP2</em>). One of the Asterid sequenced genomes, the <em>S. tuberosum</em> genome, reports a single ELIP gene (<a id="xref-ref-15-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>), similar to the pea and tobacco genome (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>), and <em>S. lycopersicum</em> has two ELIP genes (ELIP1 and ELIP2) (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">25</a>), similar to <em>Arabidopsis</em> and barley.</p></div><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>The Genome and Desiccation Tolerance.</h3><p id="p-20">To examine the response of the genome to desiccation, and to understand the architecture of its tolerance mechanisms within the genome, we profiled the dehydration-induced alteration of gene expression (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd05.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S5</a>). We constructed a genome-wide dehydration response profile by integrating the scaffold protein-coding and repetitive sequence mapping analysis with 9,888 differentially expressed genes (DEGs; identified as greater than twofold change in transcript abundances from that for hydrated controls, at a <em>P</em> value of &lt; 0.05) during drying (<a id="xref-fig-2-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>D</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd05.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S5</a>). There was no obvious clustering of DEGs, the majority of which are located, as expected, predominantly in scaffolds that contain few repetitive sequences and that are gene-rich (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd06.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S6</a>). The lack of clustering of any significant number of DEGs with their scattered location among a large number of contigs suggests DT was not acquired in a recent evolutionary or restructuring event (sufficient time for dispersal of genes throughout the genome) but, rather, as a retooling of existing genetic elements to deliver the DT phenotype in vegetative tissues.</p></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Gene Expression and Desiccation.</h3><p id="p-21">The majority of genes expressed in the leaves of <em>B. hygrometrica</em> belong to gene families. The large number of orphan genes, ∼29% of all annotated genes and 8.51–10.48% of expressed annotated genes, was within the expected range for orphan gene content of eukaryotic genomes (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S11</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-23-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>), of which only a small number (a maximum of 128) were significantly responsive to dehydration (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S11</a>). Of the 9,888 DEGs, 58.18% responded to moderate dehydration [70% relative water content (RWC)] and 87.47% responded to dehydration to 10% RWC (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>A</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd05.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S5</a>). There were 1,239 DEGs that only responded to moderate dehydration (769 increase and 470 decline), and 4,135 specifically responded during desiccation (2,188 increase and 1,947 decline).</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/18/5833/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Transcriptional responses during dehydration. IA, increased abundance; DA, decline in abundance. (B) GO classifications of the DEGs respond to dehydration and desiccation. Only GO terms with a gene number larger than 150 are shown. (C) Heat maps of significantly enriched pathways in DEGs during dehydration. The yellow and red colors indicate the Q-value for significantly enriched pathways. (D) Clusters of high-level (log2 fold change &gt; 4) DEGs during dehydration. The y axis gives the normalized expression level by DESeq software (on a log scale) of DEGs. Each blue line represents a different gene, and the red line indicates the gene expression trend of DEGs in each cluster. (E) The heat map describes the significantly enriched pathways in each cluster. The yellow and red color delineates the Q-value for significantly enriched pathways. (F) Significantly enriched pathways for those DEGs for which alternative splicing occurred during dehydration. The yellow and red color shows the Q-value for significantly enriched pathways." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1612957475" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Transcriptional responses during dehydration. The yellow and red color shows the Q-value for significantly enriched pathways.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 3." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/18/5833/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="206"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 3." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/18/5833/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="206"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/18/5833/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 3." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/112/18/5833/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/487218" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 3.</span> <p id="p-22" class="first-child">Transcriptional responses during dehydration. (<em>A</em>) Venn diagrams show the number of differentially expressed genes during dehydration and rehydration: hydrated (HD), dehydration (70% RWC), and desiccation (10% RWC). IA, increased abundance; DA, decline in abundance. (<em>B</em>) GO classifications of the DEGs respond to dehydration and desiccation. Only GO terms with a gene number larger than 150 are shown. (<em>C</em>) Heat maps of significantly enriched pathways in DEGs during dehydration. The yellow and red colors indicate the Q-value for significantly enriched pathways. (<em>D</em>) Clusters of high-level (log<sub>2</sub> fold change &gt; 4) DEGs during dehydration. The <em>y</em> axis gives the normalized expression level by DESeq software (on a log scale) of DEGs. Each blue line represents a different gene, and the red line indicates the gene expression trend of DEGs in each cluster. (<em>E</em>) The heat map describes the significantly enriched pathways in each cluster. The yellow and red color delineates the Q-value for significantly enriched pathways. (<em>F</em>) Significantly enriched pathways for those DEGs for which alternative splicing occurred during dehydration. The yellow and red color shows the Q-value for significantly enriched pathways.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-23">The assignment of GO terms for 7,716 DEGs (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd05.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S5</a>) focuses on membrane components and organelle structure, biopolymer molecular processes and intermediary metabolism, and metal binding, hydrolytic, and oxidoreductase activities (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>B</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S12</a>). Enrichment analysis of the 7,758 DEGs with KEGG annotation (<a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>C</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd05.xlsx" class="in-nw">Datasets S5</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd07.xlsx" class="in-nw">S7</a>) revealed that glycerophospholipid metabolism and soluble <em>N</em>-ethylmaleimide sensitive fusion attachment protein receptor interactions in vesicular trafficking (both processes involved in membrane maintenance) are favored during dehydration. Dehydration also favored transcripts involved in the pathogen defense system, a common observation for abiotic stress responses, and one often brokered by plant hormones [e.g., abscisic acid (ABA) (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>)]. As tissues approach desiccation, transcripts that populate the mRNA surveillance pathway appear and accumulate, indicating a need to remove damaged transcripts from the drying cells. Dehydration also resulted in depletion of transcripts that represent a wide range of metabolic processes (<a id="xref-fig-3-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>C</em></a>), primarily for pathways involved in growth (photosynthesis and nitrogen metabolism). A more focused clustering of 734 high-level DEGs revealed three major clusters (log<sub>2</sub> base mean value in one sample is more than fourfold higher than that in any other sample; <a id="xref-fig-3-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3 <em>D</em> and <em>E</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Results</em></a>, and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd08.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S8</a>), offering a broad assessment of the response to desiccation and a broad comparison with similar transcriptomes of other resurrection dicots (<a id="xref-ref-5-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>).</p><p id="p-24">This and other studies of vegetative dehydration/desiccation transcriptomes (<a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">27</a>) point toward a central core of genes and gene products associated with the ability to survive drying: ABA metabolism and signaling, phospholipid signaling, late embryogenesis abundant proteins (LEAs) (protective proteins), components of reactive oxygen species (ROS) protection and detoxification pathways, and ELIPs (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd09.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S9</a>).</p><p id="p-25">Of the 21 DEGs associated with ABA metabolism, eight positive DEGs encode enzymes directly involved in ABA biosynthesis and catabolism, indicating tight control of ABA levels during dehydration (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Results</em></a>). A single phospholipase D gene, <em>PLD-1α</em>, controlled, in part, desiccation response of the resurrection dicot <em>Craterostigma plantagineum</em> (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>). This may also be the case for <em>B. hygrometrica</em>, as evidenced by the increased abundance of transcripts from one of the two PLD-1α genes during dehydration (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd09.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S9</a>). Other <em>PLDs</em> (three <em>PLD-γs</em>, a <em>PLD-β</em>, and a <em>PLD-P1/Z1</em>) also responded positively to dehydration, indicating that phospholipid signaling may be more complex in <em>B. hygrometrica</em>.</p><p id="p-26">The <em>B. hygrometrica</em> genome contains a plethora of LEA protein genes [65 with 51 expressed and 47 DEGs (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd09.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S9</a>)], which is a much greater number than reported for the transcriptomes of <em>C. plantagineum</em> (<a id="xref-ref-27-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">27</a>) or <em>Haberlea rhodopensis</em> (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>). The greater number of expressed LEA genes may reflect the length and severity of the seasonal dehydration periods experienced by <em>Boea</em> compared with the other resurrection species (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Results</em></a>). The proteins derived from two <em>LEA1s</em>, <em>Bhs4_093</em> and <em>Bhs4_094</em>, have been demonstrated to stabilize the photosynthetic proteins (such as LHCs) in transgenic tobacco seedlings during dehydration and rehydration (<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>).</p><p id="p-27">The response to dehydration for genes involved in ROS protection and mitigation of oxidative damage is a complex one. Early studies revealed the importance of glutathione metabolism in the dehydration response of <em>Boea</em> species (<a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>). Specific members of the GST gene family responded to dehydration stress, along with several peroxidases (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd09.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S9</a>), indicative of a need for detoxification and repair of oxidative damage (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Results</em></a>).</p><p id="p-28">The increase in abundance of ELIP transcripts is a common feature of the response of DT plants to dehydration (<a id="xref-ref-4-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>), as observed in <em>H. rhodopensis</em> and <em>C. plantagineum</em> (<a id="xref-ref-27-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">27</a>, <a id="xref-ref-29-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>). Thirteen of the 17 ELIP orthologs in the <em>B. hygrometrica</em> genome were ranked as positive DEGs (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Results</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd09.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S9</a>). It thus appears that the protection of photosystem II is a major aspect of the DT mechanism for <em>B. hygrometrica</em>.</p><p id="p-29">Relating the RNA-Seq data for dehydrating to the draft genome revealed that 7,127 of the genes represent two or more alternative splicing (AS) products, delivering more functional variation than specified by the annotated gene complement alone (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd10.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S10</a>). Of the DEGs, 4,491 (45.42%) exhibited AS during dehydration (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S13</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sd11.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S11</a>). Alternative 5′ splice sites dominated the four major AS patterns. Pathway enrichment of AS-DEGs favored an increase in abundance of transcripts related to endocytosis and Fc gamma R-mediated phagocytosis, fatty acid metabolism, and peroxisomal functions, suggestive of needs for membrane component and protein removal or recycling as cells lose water, as well as an ongoing repair of membranes and removal of ROS. AS was also involved in transcript selections for the processes that were revealed in the overall analysis of DEGs mentioned previously (<a id="xref-fig-3-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>F</em></a>) (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>).</p></div></div><div class="section discussion" id="sec-5"><h2 class="">Discussion</h2><p id="p-30" class="flushleft">Vegetative DT most likely evolved in certain angiosperm lineages from selection pressures exerted by an environment that delivered lengthy periods of little or no soil water. The lack of DT-specific genome organizational features in <em>B. hygrometrica</em>, such as clustering of DEGs, supports the contention that vegetative DT evolved primarily from an alteration in the regulation of preexisting genetic modules. This most likely involved those genetic components that deliver developmentally controlled DT to seeds and pollen (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>). A portion of that alteration in the regulation of gene expression in <em>B. hygrometrica</em> clearly involves AS of transcripts and the plant hormone ABA.</p><p id="p-31">The <em>B. hygrometrica</em> genome offers some important insights into the genetic strategies used for accomplishing vegetative DT and its evolution in this resurrection species. The large number of orphan genes housed within the genome, ∼10% of expressed genes, reflects the somewhat unique nature of this resurrection species. Orphan genes are thought to represent lineage-specific adaptations and, in some plant species, to be linked to stress responses (e.g., rice) (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>). This may also be true for the expressed orphan genes of <em>B. hygrometrica</em>, but only a small number (128) can, at this point, be associated with the resurrection phenotype and probably represent species-specific aspects of the DT mechanism.</p><p id="p-32">The apparent expansion of 5S <em>rRNA</em> genes in the <em>Boea</em> lineage may reflect the need for a supply of active ribosomes during the rapid resumption of protein synthesis (and recovery) on rehydration. Because ribosomal 5S <em>rRNA</em> transcripts can only be amplified by transcription, it would seem reasonable to suggest the 5S <em>rRNA</em> gene expansion in <em>B. hygrometrica</em> evolved to meet the protein synthesis burden inherent in the resurrection phenotype. As this is the first resurrection genome, to our knowledge, to be sequenced, it remains to be seen whether this is a common genotypic feature of resurrection species.</p><p id="p-33">The genome sequence and transcriptome also revealed an expansion of the ELIP gene family in <em>B. hygrometrica</em> concomitant with enhanced transcript abundance for 13 of the 17 gene family members. ELIP proteins are postulated to protect the photosynthesis machinery from photooxidative damage by preventing the accumulation of free chlorophyll by binding pigments and preserving the chlorophyll-protein complexes (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>). ELIP proteins (and transcripts) have been reported to increase in abundance in a linear fashion with the amount of photoactivation and photodamage to the photosystem II reaction centers, D1 protein degradation, and changes in pigment level (<a id="xref-ref-24-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>). Photooxidative damage is a primary stressor for resurrection species, as they spend a considerable amount of time in the dried state and under high-light conditions (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>). Thus, it appears that <em>B. hygrometrica</em> has evolved a strategy of ELIP gene expansion to aid in its ability to protect its photosynthetic apparatus, particularly photosystem II, from oxidative damage: an essential and perhaps central aspect of its DT mechanism. The transcriptomic analysis provides a broader perspective on the nature of the cellular protection aspects of vegetative DT, highlighted by the increase in transcript abundance for LEA protein genes, GST gene family, and peroxidases.</p><p id="p-34">The draft genome offers a unique opportunity to construct a systems approach to understanding the mechanistic aspects of DT and resurrection in plants. Such an approach can help influence our understanding of the evolution of the land plants and our attempts to design strategies for the improvement of the dehydration tolerance of our major crops as food security issues increase in importance globally.</p></div><div class="section materials-methods" id="sec-6"><h2 class="">Materials and Methods</h2><p id="p-35" class="flushleft">The original accessions for <em>B. hygrometrica</em> were collected from a dry rock crack in Fragrant Hills in a Beijing suburb in China. The genome was sequenced using the whole-genome shotgun approach, using Illumina HiSeq and Roche 454 platforms. Whole-genome shotgun data were used to assemble the draft genome, using the hybrid assembly strategy by Newbler, SSPACE, and SOAP de novo algorithm. Genes were annotated using a combined approach on the repeat masked genome with ab initio gene predictions, protein similarity, and transcripts to build optimal gene models. Repeat sequences were identified by both de novo approach and sequence similarity at the nucleotide and protein levels. Detailed information of materials, methods, and any associated references are available in the <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1505811112/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1505811112.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Materials and Methods</em></a>.</p></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-36">We thank the Beijing Genetics Institute staff members and Capital Normal University graduate students for their assistances on genome sequencing, assembling, and bioinformatic analyses. This study was supported by funds from the Chinese Ministry of Agriculture (2014ZX08009-23B, 2009ZX08009-058B), Chinese Ministry of Science and Technology (2007AA021405), and the Funding Project for Academic Human Resources Development in Institutions of Higher Learning Under the Jurisdiction of Beijing Municipality (Y. He). We are also thankful for the special financial support from the National Key Disciplines of China for this project.</p></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><h2>Footnotes</h2><ul class="pnas-footnotes"><li class="corresp" id="corresp-1"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-corresp-1-1">↵</a><sup>1</sup>To whom correspondence may be addressed.
